class="plainlist" style="line-height:1.3em; padding-top:0.4em; border-top: 1px solid #aaa">\n          <ul>\n           <li>\n            <a href="/wiki/Media_of_the_United_States" title="Media of the <i>\n        <b>\n         USA Today\n        </b>\n       </i>\n       is an internationally distributed American daily\n       <a href="/wiki/Middle-market_newspaper" title="Middle-market newspaper">\n        middle-market newspaper\n       </a>\n       that serves as the flagship publication of its owner, the\n       <a href="/wiki/Gannett_Company" title="Gannett Company">\n        Gannett Company\n       </a>\n       . Founded by\n       <a href="/wiki/Al_Neuharth" title="Al Neuharth">\n        Al Neuharth\n       </a>\n       on September 15, 1982, it operates from Gannett\'s corporate headquarters on Jones Branch Drive in\n       <a href="/wiki/McLean,_Virginia" title="McLean, Virginia">\n        McLean, Virginia\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TysonsMap_3-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-TysonsMap-3">\n         [3]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       It is printed at 37 sites across the United States and at five additional sites internationally. Its dynamic design influenced the style of local, regional and national newspapers worldwide, through its use of concise reports, colorized images, informational graphics, and its inclusion of\n       <a href="/wiki/Popular_culture" title="Popular culture">\n        popular culture\n       </a>\n       stories, among other distinct features.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">\n        <a href="#cite_note-4">\n         [4]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-garcia-usatoday1_5-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-garcia-usatoday1-5">\n         [5]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       With a weekly circulation of 1,021,638 and an approximate daily reach of seven million readers as of 2016\n       <sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">\n        <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=USA_Today&amp;action=edit">\n         [update]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       ,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">\n        <a href="#cite_note-6">\n         [6]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       shares the position of having the widest\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_newspapers_in_the_United_States_by_circulation" title="List of newspapers in the United States by circulation">\n        circulation of any newspaper in the United States\n       </a>\n       with\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/The_Wall_Street_Journal" title="The Wall Street Journal">\n         The Wall Street Journal\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       and\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">\n         The New York Times\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eCirc_7-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-eCirc-7">\n         [7]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       is distributed in all 50\n       <a href="/wiki/U.S._state" title="U.S. state">\n        states\n       </a>\n       , the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/District_of_Columbia" title="District of Columbia">\n        District of Columbia\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">\n        Puerto Rico\n       </a>\n       , with an international edition distributed in\n       <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">\n        Canada\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">\n        Asia\n       </a>\n       and the Pacific Islands, and\n       <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">\n        Europe\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <p>\n      </p>\n      <div class="toc" id="toc">\n       <div class="toctitle">\n </span>\n         </a>\n        </li>\n       </ul>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">\n        History\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=USA_Today&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <p>\n       The development of\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       commenced on February 29, 1980, when company staff employed for a task force known as "Project NN" met with Gannett Company chairman Al Neuharth in\n       <a href="/wiki/Cocoa_Beach,_Florida" title="Cocoa Beach, Florida">\n        Cocoa Beach, Florida\n       </a>\n       to develop a national newspaper. Early regional prototypes included\n       <i>\n        East Bay Today\n       </i>\n       , an\n       <a href="/wiki/Oakland,_California" title="Oakland, California">\n        Oakland, California\n       </a>\n       -based publication first published in the late 1970s, to serve as the morning edition of the\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/Oakland_Tribune" title="Oakland Tribune">\n         Oakland Tribune\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       , an afternoon newspaper which Gannett owned at the time.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">\n        <a href="#cite_note-8">\n         [8]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       On June 11, 1981, Gannett printed the first prototypes of the proposed publication; the copies, which displayed two proposed design layouts, were mailed to various newsmakers and prominent leaders in journalism for review and input.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-garcia-usatoday1_5-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-garcia-usatoday1-5">\n         [5]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usatoday30.usatoday_9-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-usatoday30.usatoday-9">\n         [9]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The Gannett Company\'s Board of Directors approved the launch of the national newspaper, which would be titled\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       , on December 5, 1981; with the launch, Neuharth was appointed president and publisher of the newspaper, adding those responsibilities to his existing position as Gannett\'s\n       <a href="/wiki/Chief_executive_officer" title="Chief executive officer">\n        chief executive officer\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usatoday30.usatoday_9-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-usatoday30.usatoday-9">\n         [9]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marking30_10-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-marking30-10">\n         [10]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Gannett formally announced the launch of the paper on April 20, 1982.\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       began publishing on September 15, 1982, initially launching in the\n       <a href="/wiki/Baltimore" title="Baltimore">\n        Baltimore\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">\n        Washington, D.C.\n       </a>\n       metropolitan areas for an initial newsstand price of 25\xc2\xa2 (equivalent to 62\xc2\xa2 today). After selling out its first issue, Gannett gradually expanded the national distribution of the paper, eventually reaching an estimated circulation of 362,879 copies, double the amount of copy sales that Gannett projected that\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       would accrue by the end of 1982. The design was unique in its incorporation of colorized graphics and photographs; initially, only its front news section pages were rendered in four-color, while the remaining pages were displayed in a spot color format. The paper\'s overall content style and elevated use of graphics \xe2\x80\x93 the result of the concept developed by Neuharth, in contribution with staff graphics designers George Rorick, Sam Ward, Suzy Parker, John Sherlock and Web Bryant \xe2\x80\x93 was derided by critics, who referred to it as "\n       <a href="/wiki/McDonald%27s" title="McDonald\'s">\n        McPaper\n       </a>\n       " or "television you can wrap fish in," because it opted to incorporate more concise, shorter-form nuggets of information akin to the style of\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Television_news" title="Television news">\n        television news\n       </a>\n       rather than in-depth stories in many of its sections like traditional newspapers, which many in the newspaper industry considered to be a\n       <a href="/wiki/Dumbing_down" title="Dumbing down">\n        dumbing down\n       </a>\n       of the news.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usatoday30.usatoday_9-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-usatoday30.usatoday-9">\n         [9]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marking30_10-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-marking30-10">\n         [10]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-garcia-usatoday2_11-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-garcia-usatoday2-11">\n         [11]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Although,\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       has been in profit for ten years; it has changed the appearance and feel of most newspapers in the United States and around the world.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">\n        <a href="#cite_note-12">\n         [12]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       On July 2, 1984, the newspaper switched from a largely black-and-white to a color publication, featuring full color photography and graphics in all four sections. The following week on July 10,\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       launched an international edition intended primarily for U.S. readers abroad; this was followed four months later on October 8 with the rollout of the first via satellite publication of its international version in\n       <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">\n        Singapore\n       </a>\n       . On April 8, 1985, the paper published its first special bonus section, a 12-page section called "Baseball \'85," which previewed the\n       <a href="/wiki/1985_Major_League_Baseball_season" title="1985 Major League Baseball season">\n        1985 Major League Baseball season\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usatoday30.usatoday_9-3">\n        <a href="#cite_note-usatoday30.usatoday-9">\n         [9]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       By the fourth quarter of 1985,\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       had become the second largest newspaper in the United States, reaching a daily circulation of 1.4 million copies; total daily readership of the paper by 1987 (according to Simmons Market Research Bureau statistics) had reached 5.5 million, the largest of any daily newspaper in the U.S. On May 6, 1986,\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       began printing production of its international edition in\n       <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">\n        Switzerland\n       </a>\n       .\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       operated at a loss for most of its first four years of operation, accumulating a total deficit of $233 million after taxes, according to figures released by Gannett in July 1987; the newspaper began turning its first profit in May 1987, six months ahead of Gannett corporate revenue projections.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usatoday30.usatoday_9-4">\n        <a href="#cite_note-usatoday30.usatoday-9">\n         [9]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       On January 29, 1988,\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       published the largest edition in its history, a 78-page weekend edition featuring a section previewing\n       <a href="/wiki/Super_Bowl_XXII" title="Super Bowl XXII">\n        Super Bowl XXII\n       </a>\n       ; the edition included 44.38 pages of advertising and sold 2,114,055 copies, setting a single-day circulation record for an American newspaper (a record that the paper would beat nearly nine months later on September 2, when its\n       <a href="/wiki/Labor_Day" title="Labor Day">\n        Labor Day\n       </a>\n       weekend edition sold 2,257,734 copies). On April 15,\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       launched a third international printing site, based in\n       <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">\n        Hong Kong\n       </a>\n       . The international edition set circulation and advertising sales records during August 1988, when the publication provided extensive coverage of that year\'s\n       <a href="/wiki/1988_Summer_Olympics" title="1988 Summer Olympics">\n        Summer Olympics\n       </a>\n       , selling more than 60,000 copies and 100 pages of advertising.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usatoday30.usatoday_9-5">\n        <a href="#cite_note-usatoday30.usatoday-9">\n         [9]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       By July 1991, Simmons Market Research Bureau estimated that\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       had a total daily readership of nearly 6.6 million readers, an all-time high for the paper at the time and the largest readership of any daily newspaper in the United States. On September 1 of that year,\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       launched a fourth printsite for its international edition in\n       <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">\n        London\n       </a>\n       to publish and distribute that version of the paper to the\n       <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">\n        United Kingdom\n       </a>\n       and the\n       <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">\n        British Isles\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usatoday30.usatoday_9-6">\n        <a href="#cite_note-usatoday30.usatoday-9">\n         [9]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The international edition\'s format was changed beginning with the April 1, 1994 issue to release each issue on Monday through Fridays, rather than from Tuesday through Saturday, in order to accommodate business travelers; on February 1, 1995,\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       opened its first editorial bureau outside of the United States at its Hong Kong publishing facility; additional editorial bureaus were launched in London and\n       <a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">\n        Moscow\n       </a>\n       in 1996.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usatoday30.usatoday_9-7">\n        <a href="#cite_note-usatoday30.usatoday-9">\n         [9]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       On April 17, 1995,\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       launched its website, www.usatoday.com, as part of the USA Today Information Network to provide real-time news coverage; the site would eventually expand to include a spin-off website that launched in June 2002, USATODAY.com Travel, providing travel information and booking tools. On August 28, 1995, a fifth international publishing site was launched in\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frankfurt,_Germany" title="Frankfurt, Germany">\n        Frankfurt, Germany\n       </a>\n       , to print and distribute the international edition throughout most of Europe.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usatoday30.usatoday_9-8">\n        <a href="#cite_note-usatoday30.usatoday-9">\n         [9]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       On October 4, 1999,\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       began running advertisements on its front page for the first time.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usatoday30.usatoday_9-9">\n        <a href="#cite_note-usatoday30.usatoday-9">\n         [9]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       In 2017, some pages of USA Today\'s website features the\n       <a href="/wiki/Auto-Play" title="Auto-Play">\n        "autoplay"\n       </a>\n       functionality for video or audio-aided stories.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       On February 8, 2000, Gannett launched\n       <i>\n        USA Today Live\n       </i>\n       , a broadcast and Internet initiative designed to provide coverage from the newspaper to broadcast television stations nationwide for use in their local newscasts and their websites; the venture would also provide integration with the\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       website, which transitioned from a text-based format to feature audio and video clips of news content. The paper launched a sixth printing site for its international edition on May 15, 2000, in\n       <a href="/wiki/Milan" title="Milan">\n        Milan\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">\n        Italy\n       </a>\n       , followed on July 10 by the launch of an international printing facility in\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charleroi,_Belgium" title="Charleroi, Belgium">\n        Charleroi, Belgium\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usatoday30.usatoday_9-10">\n        <a href="#cite_note-usatoday30.usatoday-9">\n         [9]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       2001 saw additional expansion of the newspaper, with the launch of two interactive units: on June 19,\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       and Gannett Newspapers launched the USA Today Careers Network (now\n       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Careers.com&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Careers.com (page does not exist)">\n        Careers.com\n       </a>\n       ), a website featuring localized employment listings, then on July 18, the USA Today News Center was launched as an interactive television news service developed through a joint venture with the On Command Corporation that was distributed to hotels around the United States. On September 12 of that year, the newspaper set an all-time single day circulation record, selling 3,638,600 copies for its edition covering the\n       <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">\n        terrorist attacks\n       </a>\n       that destroyed the\n       <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Center_(1973%E2%80%932001)" title="World Trade Center (1973\xe2\x80\x932001)">\n        World Trade Center\n       </a>\n       and damaged\n       <a href="/wiki/The_Pentagon" title="The Pentagon">\n        The Pentagon\n       </a>\n       and a hijacking attempt tied to the two events that resulted in the crash of\n       <a href="/wiki/United_Airlines_Flight_93" title="United Airlines Flight 93">\n        United Airlines Flight 93\n       </a>\n       outside of\n       <a href="/wiki/Shanksville,_Pennsylvania" title="Shanksville, Pennsylvania">\n        Shanksville, Pennsylvania\n       </a>\n       . That November,\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       migrated its operations from Gannett\'s previous corporate headquarters in\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arlington,_Virginia" title="Arlington, Virginia">\n        Arlington, Virginia\n       </a>\n       to the company\'s new headquarters in nearby\n       <a href="/wiki/McLean,_Virginia" title="McLean, Virginia">\n        McLean\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usatoday30.usatoday_9-11">\n        <a href="#cite_note-usatoday30.usatoday-9">\n         [9]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       On December 12, 2005, Gannett announced that it would combine the separate newsroom operations of\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       <span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\n        \'\n       </span>\n       s online and print entities, with USAToday.com\'s vice president and editor-in-chief Kinsey Wilson being promoted to co-executive editor, alongside existing executive editor John Hillkirk.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usatoday30.usatoday_9-12">\n        <a href="#cite_note-usatoday30.usatoday-9">\n         [9]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       In 2010,\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       launched the USA Today\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/API" title="API">\n        API\n       </a>\n       for sharing data with partners of all types.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">\n        <a href="#cite_note-13">\n         [13]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Newsroom_restructuring_and_2011_graphical_tweaks">\n        Newsroom restructuring and 2011 graphical tweaks\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=USA_Today&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Newsroom restructuring and 2011 graphical tweaks">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       On August 27, 2010,\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       announced that it would undergo a reorganization of its newsroom, announcing the layoffs of 130 staffers. It also announced that the paper would shift its focus away from print and place more emphasis on its digital platforms (including USAToday.com and its related\n       <a href="/wiki/Mobile_app" title="Mobile app">\n        mobile applications\n       </a>\n       ) and launch of a new publication called\n       <i>\n        USA Today Sports\n       </i>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       On January 24, 2011, to reverse a revenue slide, the paper introduced a tweaked format that modified the appearance of its front section pages, which included a larger logo at the top of each page; coloring tweaks to section front pages; a new\n       <a href="/wiki/Sans-serif" title="Sans-serif">\n        sans-serif\n       </a>\n       font, called Prelo, for certain headlines of main stories (replacing the Gulliver typeface that had been implemented for story headers in April 2000); an updated "Newsline" feature featuring larger, "newsier" headline entry points; and the increasing and decreasing of\n       <a href="/wiki/Masthead_(publishing)" title="Masthead (publishing)">\n        mastheads\n       </a>\n       and white space to present a cleaner style.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">\n        <a href="#cite_note-14">\n         [14]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">\n        <a href="#cite_note-15">\n         [15]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="2012_redesign">\n        2012 redesign\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=USA_Today&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 2012 redesign">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Miguel_Vazquez_from_USA_Today_shows_off_their_beautiful_Metro_App_(6857362418).jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1566" data-file-width="2433" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Miguel_Vazquez_from_USA_Today_shows_off_their_beautiful_Metro_App_%286857362418%29.jpg/220px-Miguel_Vazquez_from_USA_Today_shows_off_their_beautiful_Metro_App_%286857362418%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Miguel_Vazquez_from_USA_Today_shows_off_their_beautiful_Metro_App_%286857362418%29.jpg/330px-Miguel_Vazquez_from_USA_Today_shows_off_their_beautiful_Metro_App_%286857362418%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Miguel_Vazquez_from_USA_Today_shows_off_their_beautiful_Metro_App_%286857362418%29.jpg/440px-Miguel_Vazquez_from_USA_Today_shows_off_their_beautiful_Metro_App_%286857362418%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Miguel_Vazquez_from_USA_Today_shows_off_their_beautiful_Metro_App_(6857362418).jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         Miguel Vazquez from\n         <i>\n          USA Today\n         </i>\n         shows off the publication\'s Metro App.\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       On September 14, 2012,\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       underwent the first major redesign in its history, in commemoration for the 30th anniversary of the paper\'s first edition.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">\n        <a href="#cite_note-16">\n         [16]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Developed in conjunction with brand design firm\n       <a href="/wiki/Wolff_Olins" title="Wolff Olins">\n        Wolff Olins\n       </a>\n       , the print edition of\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       added a page covering technology stories and expanded travel coverage within the Life section and increased the number of color pages included in each edition, while retaining longtime elements.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hagey_17-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Hagey-17">\n         [17]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The "globe" logo used since the paper\'s inception was replaced with a new logo featuring a large circle rendered in colors corresponding to each of the sections, serving as an infographic that changes with news stories, containing images representing that day\'s top stories.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hagey_17-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Hagey-17">\n         [17]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-garcia-usatoday5_18-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-garcia-usatoday5-18">\n         [18]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The paper\'s website was also extensively overhauled using a new, in-house\n       <a href="/wiki/Content_management_system" title="Content management system">\n        content management system\n       </a>\n       known as Presto and a design created by Fantasy Interactive, that incorporates flipboard-style navigation to switch between individual stories (which obscure most of the main and section pages), clickable video advertising and a\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Responsive_design" title="Responsive design">\n        responsive design\n       </a>\n       layout. The site was designed to be more interactive, provide optimizations for\n       <a href="/wiki/Mobile_device" title="Mobile device">\n        mobile\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Touchscreen" title="Touchscreen">\n        touchscreen\n       </a>\n       devices, provide "high impact" advertising units, and provide the ability for Gannett to syndicate USA Today content to the websites of its local properties, and vice versa. To accomplish this goal, Gannett migrated its newspaper and television station websites to the Presto platform and the USA Today site design throughout 2013 and 2014 (although archive content accessible through\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Search_engines" title="Search engines">\n        search engines\n       </a>\n       remains available through the pre-relaunch design).\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-poynter-gannettpresto_19-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-poynter-gannettpresto-19">\n         [19]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-garcia-usatoday6_20-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-garcia-usatoday6-20">\n         [20]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Mid-2010s_expansion_and_restructuring">\n        Mid-2010s expansion and restructuring\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=USA_Today&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Mid-2010s expansion and restructuring">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       On October 6, 2013, Gannett test launched a daily "butterfly" edition of\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       for distribution as an insert in four of its newspapers \xe2\x80\x93\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/The_Indianapolis_Star" title="The Indianapolis Star">\n         The Indianapolis Star\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       , the\n       <i>\n        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rochester_Democrat_%26_Chronicle" title="Rochester Democrat &amp; Chronicle">\n         Rochester Democrat &amp; Chronicle\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       , the\n       <a href="/wiki/Fort_Myers,_Florida" title="Fort Myers, Florida">\n        Fort Myers\n       </a>\n       -based\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/The_News-Press" title="The News-Press">\n         News-Press\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       and the\n       <a href="/wiki/Appleton,_Wisconsin" title="Appleton, Wisconsin">\n        Appleton, Wisconsin\n       </a>\n       -based\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/The_Post-Crescent" title="The Post-Crescent">\n         Post-Crescent\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       . The launch of the syndicated insert caused\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       to restructure its operations to allow seven-day-a-week production to accommodate the packaging of its national and international news content and enterprise stories (comprising about 10 pages for the weekday and Saturday editions, and up to 22 pages for the Sunday edition) into the pilot insert. Gannett later announced on December 11, that it would formally launch the condensed daily edition of\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       in 31 additional local newspapers nationwide through April 2014 (with the\n       <a href="/wiki/Palm_Springs,_California" title="Palm Springs, California">\n        Palm Springs, California\n       </a>\n       -based\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/The_Desert_Sun" title="The Desert Sun">\n         Desert Sun\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       and the\n       <a href="/wiki/Lafayette,_Louisiana" title="Lafayette, Louisiana">\n        Lafayette, Louisiana\n       </a>\n       -based\n       <i>\n        Advertiser\n       </i>\n       being the first newspapers outside of the pilot program participants to add the supplement on December 15), citing "positive feedback" to the feature from readers and advertisers of the initial four papers. Gannett was given permission from the\n       <a href="/wiki/Alliance_for_Audited_Media" title="Alliance for Audited Media">\n        Alliance for Audited Media\n       </a>\n       to count the circulation figures from the syndicated local insert with the total circulation count for the flagship national edition of\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">\n        <a href="#cite_note-21">\n         [21]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">\n        <a href="#cite_note-22">\n         [22]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       On January 4, 2014,\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       acquired the book and film review website, Reviewed.com.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usatoday30.usatoday_9-13">\n        <a href="#cite_note-usatoday30.usatoday-9">\n         [9]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       In the first quarter of 2014, Gannett launched a condensed\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       insert into 31 other newspapers in its network, thereby increasing the number of inserts to 35, in an effort to shore up\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       <span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\n        \'\n       </span>\n       s circulation after it regained its position as the highest circulated weekdaily newspaper in the United States in October 2013.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USA_Today_distribution_23-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-USA_Today_distribution-23">\n         [23]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       On September 3, 2014,\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       announced that it would lay off roughly 70 employees in a restructuring of its newsroom and business operations.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">\n        <a href="#cite_note-24">\n         [24]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       In October 2014,\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       and OpenWager Inc. entered into a partnership to release a\n       <a href="/wiki/Online_bingo#Mobile_Bingo" title="Online bingo">\n        Bingo\n       </a>\n       app called USA TODAY Bingo Cruise.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">\n        <a href="#cite_note-25">\n         [25]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       On December 3, 2015, Gannett formally launched the USA Today Network, a national digital newsgathering service providing shared content between\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       and the company\'s 92 local newspapers throughout the United States as well as pooling advertising services on both a hyperlocal and national reach. The\n       <i>\n        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louisville_Courier-Journal" title="Louisville Courier-Journal">\n         Louisville Courier-Journal\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       had earlier soft-launched the service as part of a pilot program started on November 17, coinciding with an imaging rebrand for the\n       <a href="/wiki/Louisville,_Kentucky" title="Louisville, Kentucky">\n        Louisville, Kentucky\n       </a>\n       -based newspaper; Gannett\'s other local newspaper properties, as well as those it acquired through its merger with the\n       <a href="/wiki/Journal_Media_Group" title="Journal Media Group">\n        Journal Media Group\n       </a>\n       , began identifying themselves as part of the USA Today Network (foregoing use of the Gannett name outside of requisite ownership references) gradually integrated into the USA Today Network through early January 2016.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">\n        <a href="#cite_note-26">\n         [26]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">\n        <a href="#cite_note-27">\n         [27]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">\n        <a href="#cite_note-28">\n         [28]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Layout_and_format">\n        Layout and format\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=USA_Today&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Layout and format">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:USA_Today_cover_page.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="419" data-file-width="237" height="389" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e9/USA_Today_cover_page.jpg/220px-USA_Today_cover_page.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/e/e9/USA_Today_cover_page.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:USA_Today_cover_page.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         This February 5, 2009 issue of\n         <i>\n          USA Today\n         </i>\n         shows the old layout and logo of the paper prior to its 2012 redesign.\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       is known for synthesizing news down to easy-to-read-and-comprehend stories. In the main edition circulated in the United States and some\n       <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">\n        Canadian\n       </a>\n       cities, each edition consists of four sections: News (the oft-labeled "front page" section), Money, Sports, and Life. Since March 1998, the Friday edition of Life has been separated into two distinct sections: the regular Life focusing on entertainment (subtitled\n       <i>\n        Weekend\n       </i>\n       ; section E), which features television reviews and listings, a DVD column,\n       <a href="/wiki/Film_criticism" title="Film criticism">\n        film reviews\n       </a>\n       and trends, and a travel supplement called\n       <i>\n        Destinations &amp; Diversions\n       </i>\n       (section D). The international edition of the paper features two sections: News and Money in one; with Sports and Life in the other.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Atypical of most daily newspapers, the paper does not print on Saturdays and Sundays; the Friday edition serves as the weekend edition (although\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       has published special Saturday and Sunday editions in the past, the first being published on January 19, 1991, when it released a Saturday "Extra" edition updating coverage of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Gulf_War" title="Gulf War">\n        Gulf War\n       </a>\n       from the previous day; the paper published special seven-day-a-week editions for the first time on July 19, 1996, when it published special editions for exclusive distribution in the host city of\n       <a href="/wiki/Atlanta" title="Atlanta">\n        Atlanta\n       </a>\n       and surrounding areas for the two-week duration of the\n       <a href="/wiki/1996_Summer_Olympics" title="1996 Summer Olympics">\n        Summer Olympics\n       </a>\n       ).\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usatoday30.usatoday_9-14">\n        <a href="#cite_note-usatoday30.usatoday-9">\n         [9]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       prints each complete story on the front page of the respective section with the exception of the cover story. The cover story is a longer story that requires a jump (readers must turn to another page in the paper to complete the story, usually the next page of that section). On certain days, the news or sports section will take up two paper sections, and there will be a second cover story within the second section.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Each section is denoted by a certain color to differentiate sections beyond lettering and is seen in a box the top-left corner of the first page; the principal section colors are blue for News (section A), green for Money (section B), red for Sports (section C), and purple for Life (section D); in the paper\'s early years, the Life and Money sections were also assigned blue nameplates and spot color, as the presses used at\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       <span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\n        \'\n       </span>\n       printing facilities did not yet accommodate the use of other colors to denote all four original sections.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-garcia-usatoday4_29-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-garcia-usatoday4-29">\n         [29]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Orange is used for bonus sections (section E or above), which are published occasionally such as for\n       <a href="/wiki/Business_travel" title="Business travel">\n        business travel\n       </a>\n       trends and the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olympics" title="Olympics">\n        Olympics\n       </a>\n       ; other bonus sections for sports (such as for the\n       <a href="/wiki/PGA_Tour" title="PGA Tour">\n        PGA Tour\n       </a>\n       preview,\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NCAA_Men%27s_Division_I_Basketball_Championship" title="NCAA Men\'s Division I Basketball Championship">\n        NCAA Basketball Tournaments\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Memorial_Day" title="Memorial Day">\n        Memorial Day\n       </a>\n       auto races (\n       <a href="/wiki/Indianapolis_500" title="Indianapolis 500">\n        Indianapolis 500\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola_600" title="Coca-Cola 600">\n        Coca-Cola 600\n       </a>\n       ),\n       <a href="/wiki/National_Football_League" title="National Football League">\n        NFL\n       </a>\n       opening weekend and the\n       <a href="/wiki/Super_Bowl" title="Super Bowl">\n        Super Bowl\n       </a>\n       ) previously used the orange color, but now use the red designated for sports in their bonus sections. To increase their ties to\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       , Gannett incorporated the\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       coloring scheme into an internally created graphics package for news programming that the company began phasing in across its television station group \xe2\x80\x93 which were spun-off in July 2015 into the separate broadcast and digital media company\n       <a href="/wiki/Tegna,_Inc." title="Tegna, Inc.">\n        Tegna\n       </a>\n       \xe2\x80\x93 in late 2012 (the package utilizes the color scheme for a rundown graphic used on most stations \xe2\x80\x93 outside of those that Gannett acquired in 2014 from London Broadcasting, which began implementing the package in late 2015 \xe2\x80\x93 that persists throughout its stations\' newscasts, as well as bumpers for individual story topics). Gannett\'s television stations began to a new on-air appearance that uses a color-coding system identical to that of the paper.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tvnc-newgraphics_30-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-tvnc-newgraphics-30">\n         [30]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <div class="thumb tleft">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:177px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:USA_Today_Logo.svg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="266" data-file-width="425" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/8f/USA_Today_Logo.svg/175px-USA_Today_Logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/8f/USA_Today_Logo.svg/263px-USA_Today_Logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/8f/USA_Today_Logo.svg/350px-USA_Today_Logo.svg.png 2x" width="175"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:USA_Today_Logo.svg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         Original logo, used from 1982 to 2012.\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       In many ways,\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       is set up to break the typical newspaper layout. Some examples of that divergence from tradition include using the left-hand quarter of each section as reefers (front-page paragraphs referring to stories on inside pages\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">\n        <a href="#cite_note-31">\n         [31]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       ), sometimes using sentence-length blurbs to describe stories inside; the lead reefer is the cover page feature "Newsline," which shows summarized descriptions of headline stories featured in all four main sections and any special sections. As a national newspaper,\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       cannot focus on the weather for any one city. Therefore, the entire back page of the News section is used for weather maps for the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Continental_United_States" title="Continental United States">\n        continental United States\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">\n        Puerto Rico\n       </a>\n       and the\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Virgin_Islands" title="United States Virgin Islands">\n        United States Virgin Islands\n       </a>\n       , and temperature lists for many cities throughout the U.S. and the world (temperatures for individual cities on the primary forecast map and temperature lists are suffixed with a one- or two-letter code, such as "t" for\n       <a href="/wiki/Thunderstorm" title="Thunderstorm">\n        thunderstorms\n       </a>\n       , referencing the expected weather conditions); the colorized forecast map, originally created by staff designer George Rorick (who left\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       for a similar position at\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/The_Detroit_News" title="The Detroit News">\n         The Detroit News\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       in 1986), was copied by newspapers around the world, breaking from the traditional style of using monochrome contouring or simplistic text to denote temperature ranges.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-garcia-usatoday2_11-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-garcia-usatoday2-11">\n         [11]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-garcia-usatoday3_32-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-garcia-usatoday3-32">\n         [32]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       National precipitation maps for the next three days (previously five days until the 2012 redesign), and four-day forecasts and\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air_Quality_Index" title="Air Quality Index">\n        Air Quality Indexes\n       </a>\n       for 36 major U.S. cities (originally 16 cities prior to 1999) \xe2\x80\x93 with individual cities color-coded by the temperature contour corresponding to the given area on the forecast map \xe2\x80\x93 are also featured. Weather data is provided by\n       <a href="/wiki/AccuWeather" title="AccuWeather">\n        AccuWeather\n       </a>\n       , which has served as the forecast provider for\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       for most of the paper\'s existence (with an exception from January 2002 to September 2012, when\n       <a href="/wiki/The_Weather_Channel" title="The Weather Channel">\n        The Weather Channel\n       </a>\n       provided data through a long-term multimedia content agreement with Gannett).\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">\n        <a href="#cite_note-33">\n         [33]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">\n        <a href="#cite_note-34">\n         [34]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">\n        <a href="#cite_note-35">\n         [35]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">\n        <a href="#cite_note-36">\n         [36]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">\n        <a href="#cite_note-37">\n         [37]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       In the bottom left-hand corner of the weather page is "Weather Focus", a graphic which explains various meteorological phenomena. On some days, the Weather Focus could be a photo of a rare meteorological event.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       On Mondays, the Money section uses its back page for "Market Trends," a feature that launched in June 2002 and presents an unusual graphic depicting the performance of various industry groups as a function of quarterly, monthly, and weekly movements against the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S%26P_500" title="S&amp;P 500">\n        S&amp;P 500\n       </a>\n       . On days featuring bonus sections or business holidays, the Money and Life sections are usually combined into one section, while combinations of the Friday Life editions into one section are common during quiet weeks. Advertising coverage is seen in the Monday Money section, which often includes a review of a current television ad, and after\n       <a href="/wiki/Super_Bowl_Sunday" title="Super Bowl Sunday">\n        Super Bowl Sunday\n       </a>\n       , a review of the ads aired during the broadcast with the results of the\n       <i>\n        Ad Track\n       </i>\n       live survey. Stock tables for individual stock exchanges (comprising one subsection for companies traded on the\n       <a href="/wiki/New_York_Stock_Exchange" title="New York Stock Exchange">\n        New York Stock Exchange\n       </a>\n       , and another for companies trading on\n       <a href="/wiki/NASDAQ" title="NASDAQ">\n        NASDAQ\n       </a>\n       and the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_Stock_Exchange" title="American Stock Exchange">\n        American Stock Exchange\n       </a>\n       ) and mutual indexes were discontinued with the 2012 redesign due to the myriad of electronic ways to check individual stock prices, in line with most newspapers.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Book coverage, including reviews and a national sales chart (the latter of which debuted on October 28, 1994), is seen on Thursdays in Life, with the official full\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A.C._Nielsen" title="A.C. Nielsen">\n        A.C. Nielsen\n       </a>\n       <a href="/wiki/Nielsen_ratings" title="Nielsen ratings">\n        television ratings\n       </a>\n       chart printed on Wednesdays or Thursdays, depending on release. The paper also publishes the\n       <a href="/wiki/Mediabase" title="Mediabase">\n        Mediabase\n       </a>\n       survey for several genres of music, based on radio airplay spins on Tuesdays, along with their own chart of the top ten singles in general on Wednesdays. Because of the same limitations cited for its nationalized forecasts, the television page in Life \xe2\x80\x93 which provides\n       <a href="/wiki/Prime_time" title="Prime time">\n        prime time\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Late_night_television" title="Late night television">\n        late night\n       </a>\n       listings (running from 8:00\xc2\xa0p.m. to 12:30\xc2\xa0a.m.\n       <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Time_Zone" title="Eastern Time Zone">\n        Eastern Time\n       </a>\n       ) \xe2\x80\x93 incorporates a boilerplate "\n       <a href="/wiki/Local_news" title="Local news">\n        Local news\n       </a>\n       " or "\n       <a href="/wiki/Local_programming" title="Local programming">\n        Local programming\n       </a>\n       " descriptions to denote time periods in which the five major English language broadcast networks (\n       <a href="/wiki/American_Broadcasting_Company" title="American Broadcasting Company">\n        ABC\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/NBC" title="NBC">\n        NBC\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/CBS" title="CBS">\n        CBS\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Fox_Broadcasting_Company" title="Fox Broadcasting Company">\n        Fox\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/The_CW" title="The CW">\n        The CW\n       </a>\n       ) cede airtime to allow their affiliates to carry\n       <a href="/wiki/Broadcast_syndication" title="Broadcast syndication">\n        syndicated programs\n       </a>\n       or local newscasts; the television page has never been accompanied by a weekly listings supplement with broader scheduling information similar to those featured in local newspapers. Like most national papers,\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       does not carry\n       <a href="/wiki/Comic_strip" title="Comic strip">\n        comic strips\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <div class="thumb tleft">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:USA_Today_building.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1716" data-file-width="2916" height="129" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/USA_Today_building.jpg/220px-USA_Today_building.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/USA_Today_building.jpg/330px-USA_Today_building.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/USA_Today_building.jpg/440px-USA_Today_building.jpg 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:USA_Today_building.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         <i>\n          USA Today\n         </i>\n         is headquartered in\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tysons_Corner,_Virginia" title="Tysons Corner, Virginia">\n          Tysons Corner, Virginia\n         </a>\n         .\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       One of the staples of the News section is "Across the USA," a state-by-state roundup of headlines. The summaries consist of paragraph-length\n       <a href="/wiki/Associated_Press" title="Associated Press">\n        Associated Press\n       </a>\n       reports highlighting one story of note in each state, the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/District_of_Columbia" title="District of Columbia">\n        District of Columbia\n       </a>\n       , and one U.S. territory. Similarly, the "For the Record" page of the Sports section (which features sports scores for both the previous four days of league play and individual non-league events, seasonal league statistics and wagering lines for the current day\'s games) also features a rundown of winning numbers from the previous deadline date for\n       <a href="/wiki/Lotteries_in_the_United_States" title="Lotteries in the United States">\n        all participating state lotteries\n       </a>\n       and individual multi-state lotteries.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Some traditions have been retained, however. The\n       <a href="/wiki/News_style" title="News style">\n        lead story\n       </a>\n       still appears on the upper-right hand of the front page. Commentary and political cartoons occupy the last few pages of the News section. Stock and mutual fund data are presented in the Money section. But\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       is sufficiently different in aesthetics to be recognized on sight, even in a mix of other newspapers, such as at a\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newsstand" title="Newsstand">\n        newsstand\n       </a>\n       . The overall design and layout of\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       has been described as neo-Victorian.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">\n        <a href="#cite_note-38">\n         [38]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Also, in most of the sections\' front pages, on the lower left hand corner, are "USA Today Snapshots", which give statistics of various lifestyle interests according to the section it is in (for example, a snapshot in "Life" could show how many people tend to watch a certain genre of television show based upon the type of mood they are in at the time). These "Snapshots" are shown through graphs which are made up of various illustrations of objects that roughly pertain to the graphs subject matter (using the example above, the graph\'s bars could be made up of several TV sets, or ended by one). These are usually loosely based on research by a national institute (with the credited source mentioned in fine print in the box below the graph).\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The newspaper also features an occasional magazine supplement called\n       <i>\n        Open Air\n       </i>\n       , which launched on March 7, 2008 and appears several times a year. Various other\n       <a href="/wiki/Advertorial" title="Advertorial">\n        advertorials\n       </a>\n       appear throughout the year, mainly on Fridays.\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Opinion_section">\n        Opinion section\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=USA_Today&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Opinion section">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       The opinion section prints\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       editorials, columns by guest writers and members of the Editorial Board of Contributors,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">\n        <a href="#cite_note-39">\n         [39]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       letters to the editor, and editorial cartoons. One unique feature of the\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       editorial page is the publication of opposing points of view; alongside the editorial board\'s piece on the day\'s topic runs an opposing view by a guest writer, often an expert in the field. The opinion pieces featured in each edition are decided by the Board of Contributors, which are separate from the paper\'s news staff.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usatoday_40-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-usatoday-40">\n         [40]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       As of 2010\n       <sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">\n        <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=USA_Today&amp;action=edit">\n         [update]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       , the editorial page editor was Brian Gallagher, who has worked for the newspaper since its founding in 1982. Other members of the Editorial Board included deputy editorial page editor Bill Sternberg, executive forum editor John Siniff, op-ed/forum page editor Glen Nishimura, operations editor Thuan Le Elston, letters editor Michelle Poblete, web content editor Eileen Rivers, and editorial writers Dan Carney, George Hager, and Saundra Torry.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">\n        <a href="#cite_note-41">\n         [41]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The newspaper\'s website calls this group "demographically and ideologically diverse."\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usatoday_40-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-usatoday-40">\n         [40]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       has traditionally maintained a policy not to endorse candidates for the\n       <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">\n        United States Presidency\n       </a>\n       or any other state or federal political office, which it has refrained from doing since its inception. Since\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1984" title="United States presidential election, 1984">\n        1984\n       </a>\n       , its political editorials during the Presidential election cycle has focused instead on providing opinion on major issues relevant to the campaign based on the differing concerns of voters, the vast amount of information on ongoing Presidential campaigns, and the Board of Contributors\' aim to provide a fair viewpoint through the diverse political ideologies of its members and avoid reader perceptions of bias. However, the board re-evaluates its non-endorsement policy through an independent process during each four-year election cycle, with any decision to circumvent the policy based on a consensus vote in which fewer than two of the editorial board\'s members dissent or hold differing opinions.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usatoday-breakingtradition_42-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-usatoday-breakingtradition-42">\n         [42]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The editorial board broke from this stance for the first time on September 29, 2016, when it published an op-ed piece condemning the candidacy of\n       <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">\n        Republican\n       </a>\n       nominee\n       <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">\n        Donald Trump\n       </a>\n       , calling him "unfit for the presidency" due to his inflammatory campaign rhetoric (particularly that aimed at military veterans, immigrants, and various ethnic and religious groups); his temperament and lack of financial transparency; his "checkered" business record; his use of false and hyperbolic statements; the inconsistency of his viewpoints and issues with his vision on domestic and foreign policy; and, based on comments he has made during his campaign and criticisms by both\n       <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">\n        Democrats\n       </a>\n       and Republicans on these views, the potential risks to national security and constitutional ethics under a Trump administration, asking voters to "resist the siren song of a dangerous demagogue".\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">\n        <a href="#cite_note-43">\n         [43]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The board noted that the piece was not a "qualified endorsement" of Democratic nominee\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hillary_Rodham_Clinton" title="Hillary Rodham Clinton">\n        Hillary Clinton\n       </a>\n       , for whom the board was unable to reach a consensus for endorsing (some editorial board members expressed that Clinton\'s public service record would help her "serve the nation ably as its president," while others had "serious reservations about [her] sense of entitlement, [...] lack of candor and [...]\n       <a href="/wiki/Hillary_Clinton_email_controversy" title="Hillary Clinton email controversy">\n        extreme carelessness in handling classified information\n       </a>\n       "), instead advising voters to decide whether to vote for Clinton,\n       <a href="/wiki/Libertarian_Party_(United_States)" title="Libertarian Party (United States)">\n        Libertarian\n       </a>\n       nominee\n       <a href="/wiki/Gary_Johnson" title="Gary Johnson">\n        Gary Johnson\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Green_Party_of_the_United_States" title="Green Party of the United States">\n        Green Party\n       </a>\n       nominee\n       <a href="/wiki/Jill_Stein" title="Jill Stein">\n        Jill Stein\n       </a>\n       or a write-in candidate; or focus on Senate, House and other down-ballot political races.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">\n        <a href="#cite_note-44">\n         [44]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">\n        <a href="#cite_note-45">\n         [45]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">\n        <a href="#cite_note-46">\n         [46]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Personnel">\n        Personnel\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=USA_Today&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Personnel">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <p>\n       In May 2012, Larry Kramer \xe2\x80\x93 a 40-year media industry veteran and former president of CBS Digital Media \xe2\x80\x93 was appointed president and publisher of\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       , replacing\n       <a href="/wiki/David_Hunke" title="David Hunke">\n        David Hunke\n       </a>\n       , who had been publisher of the newspaper since 2009.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">\n        <a href="#cite_note-47">\n         [47]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Kramer was tasked with developing a new strategy for the paper as it sought to increase revenue from its digital operations.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">\n        <a href="#cite_note-48">\n         [48]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       In July 2012, Kramer hired\n       <a href="/wiki/David_Callaway_(USA_Today)" title="David Callaway (USA Today)">\n        David Callaway\n       </a>\n       \xe2\x80\x93 whom the former had hired as lead editor of\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/MarketWatch" title="MarketWatch">\n         MarketWatch\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       in 1999, two years after Kramer founded the website during his tenure at\n       <a href="/wiki/CBS_News" title="CBS News">\n        CBS News\n       </a>\n       \xe2\x80\x93 as the paper\'s editor-in-chief. Callaway had previously worked at\n       <a href="/wiki/Bloomberg_L.P." title="Bloomberg L.P.">\n        Bloomberg\n       </a>\n       covering the banking, investment-banking and asset-management businesses throughout Europe and at the\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/Boston_Herald" title="Boston Herald">\n         Boston Herald\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       , where he co-wrote a daily financial column on "comings and goings in the Boston business district".\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">\n        <a href="#cite_note-49">\n         [49]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conservative_(politics)" title="Conservative (politics)">\n        Conservative\n       </a>\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Political_activism" title="Political activism">\n        activist\n       </a>\n       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Peter_Gemma&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Peter Gemma (page does not exist)">\n        Peter Gemma\n       </a>\n       has written more than 100 op-ed pieces for\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">\n        <a href="#cite_note-50">\n         [50]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The current Editor-in-Chief is Patty Michalski.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usatoday-about_1-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-usatoday-about-1">\n         [1]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usatoday_staff2016_2-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-usatoday_staff2016-2">\n         [2]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Editorial_board">\n        Editorial board\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=USA_Today&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Editorial board">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <ul>\n       <li>\n        <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Bill_Sternberg&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Bill Sternberg (page does not exist)">\n         Bill Sternberg\n        </a>\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=David_Mastio&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="David Mastio (page does not exist)">\n         David Mastio\n        </a>\n        \xe2\x80\x93 said libertarian pundit\n        <a href="/wiki/John_Stossel" title="John Stossel">\n         John Stossel\n        </a>\n        had a conflict of interest\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Jill_Lawrence&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Jill Lawrence (page does not exist)">\n         Jill Lawrence\n        </a>\n        \xe2\x80\x93 see\n        <a href="/wiki/Politics_Daily" title="Politics Daily">\n         Politics Daily\n        </a>\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Dan_Carney&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Dan Carney (page does not exist)">\n         Dan Carney\n        </a>\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Thuan_Le_Elston&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Thuan Le Elston (page does not exist)">\n         Thuan Le Elston\n        </a>\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Josh_Rivera&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Josh Rivera (page does not exist)">\n         Josh Rivera\n        </a>\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Eileen_Rivers&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Eileen Rivers (page does not exist)">\n         Eileen Rivers\n        </a>\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Saundra_Torry&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Saundra Torry (page does not exist)">\n         Saundra Torry\n        </a>\n        \xe2\x80\x93 active in the\n        <a href="/wiki/Reporters_Committee_for_Freedom_of_the_Press" title="Reporters Committee for Freedom of the Press">\n         Reporters Committee for Freedom of the Press\n        </a>\n        since 2000\n       </li>\n      </ul>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Related_publications_and_services">\n        Related publications and services\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=USA_Today&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Related publications and services">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="USA_Weekend">\n        <i>\n         USA Weekend\n        </i>\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=USA_Today&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: USA Weekend">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/USA_Weekend" title="USA Weekend">\n        USA Weekend\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       <i>\n        <b>\n         USA Weekend\n        </b>\n       </i>\n       is a defunct sister publication that launched in 1953 as\n       <i>\n        Family Weekly\n       </i>\n       , a national\n       <a href="/wiki/Sunday_magazine" title="Sunday magazine">\n        weekend newsmagazine\n       </a>\n       supplement intended for the Sunday editions of various U.S. newspapers; it adopted its final title following Gannett\'s purchase of the magazine in 1985.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Family_51-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Family-51">\n         [51]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The magazine \xe2\x80\x93 which was distributed to approximately 800 newspapers nationwide at its peak with most Gannett-owned local newspapers carrying it by default within their Sunday editions \xe2\x80\x93 focused primarily on social issues, entertainment, health, food and travel.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Family_51-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Family-51">\n         [51]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-portada_52-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-portada-52">\n         [52]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       On December 5, 2014, Gannett announced that it would cease publishing\n       <i>\n        USA Weekend\n       </i>\n       after the December 26\xe2\x80\x9328 edition, citing increasing operational costs and reduced advertising revenue, with most of its participating newspapers choosing to replace it with competing Sunday magazine\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/Parade_(magazine)" title="Parade (magazine)">\n         Parade\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes_53-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-nytimes-53">\n         [53]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-parade_54-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-parade-54">\n         [54]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wrap_55-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-wrap-55">\n         [55]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adweek_56-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-adweek-56">\n         [56]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="USA_Today_Sports_Weekly">\n        <i>\n         USA Today Sports Weekly\n        </i>\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=USA_Today&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: USA Today Sports Weekly">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/Sports_Weekly" title="Sports Weekly">\n        Sports Weekly\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       <i>\n        <b>\n         USA Today Sports Weekly\n        </b>\n       </i>\n       is a weekly magazine that covers news and statistics from\n       <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball" title="Major League Baseball">\n        Major League Baseball\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Minor_League_Baseball" title="Minor League Baseball">\n        minor league\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/National_Collegiate_Athletic_Association" title="National Collegiate Athletic Association">\n        NCAA\n       </a>\n       baseball, the\n       <a href="/wiki/National_Football_League" title="National Football League">\n        National Football League\n       </a>\n       (NFL) and\n       <a href="/wiki/NASCAR" title="NASCAR">\n        NASCAR\n       </a>\n       . It was first published on April 5, 1991 as\n       <i>\n        USA Today Baseball Weekly\n       </i>\n       , a\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tabloid_(paper_size)" title="Tabloid (paper size)">\n        tabloid\n       </a>\n       -sized baseball-focused publication released on Wednesdays, on a weekly basis during the baseball season and bi-weekly during the off-season; the magazine expanded its sports coverage on September 4, 2002, when it adopted its current title after added stories about the NFL.\n       <i>\n        Sports Weekly\n       </i>\n       added coverage of NASCAR on February 15, 2006, lasting only during that year\'s race season; and added coverage of NCAA college football on August 8, 2007. The editorial operations of\n       <i>\n        Sports Weekly\n       </i>\n       originally operated autonomously from\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       , before being integrated with the newspaper\'s sports department in late 2005.\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="The_Big_Lead">\n        The Big Lead\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=USA_Today&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: The Big Lead">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/The_Big_Lead" title="The Big Lead">\n        The Big Lead\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       <b>\n        The Big Lead\n       </b>\n       is a sports\n       <a href="/wiki/Blog" title="Blog">\n        blog\n       </a>\n       operated by\n       <i>\n        USA Today\n       </i>\n       that was launched in February 2006 by original owner Fantasy Sports Ventures (co-founded by Jason McIntyre and David Lessa), which was purchased by the Gannett Company \xe2\x80\x93 which, beginning in April 2008, had maintained a strategic content and marketing partnership with the former company \xe2\x80\x93 in January 2012.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">\n        <a href="#cite_note-57">\n         [57]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The site \xe2\x80\x93 which is usually updated on a routine basis of 10 to 15 times per day between 8:00\xc2\xa0a.m. and 6:00\xc2\xa0p.m. Eastern Time \xe2\x80\x93 mainly covers sports, but also provides news and commentary on other news topics, ranging from politics to\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pop_culture" title="Pop culture">\n        pop culture\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">\n        <a href="#cite_note-58">\n         [58]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="USA_Today:_The_Television_Show">\n        <i>\n         USA Today: The Television Show\n        </i>\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=USA_Today&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: USA Today: The Television Show">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <table class="infobox vevent" style="width:22em">\n       <tr>\n        <th class="summary" colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold;font-style: italic; background: #CCCCFF; padding: 0.25em 1em; line-height: 1.5em;">\n         USA Today: The Television Show\n        </th>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <th scope="row">\n         Also known as\n        </th>\n        <td>\n         <div class="plainlist">\n          <ul>\n           <li>\n            <i>\n             USA Today on TV\n            </i>\n           </li>\n           <li>\n            <i>\n             USA Today\n            </i>\n           </li>\n          </ul>\n         </div>\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <th scope="row">\n         Created by\n        </th>\n        <td>\n         <a href="/wiki/Grant_Tinker" title="Grant Tinker">\n          Grant Tinker\n         </a>\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <th scope="row">\n         Country of origin\n        </th>\n        <td>\n         United States\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <th scope="row">\n         Original\n         <span class="nowrap">\n          language(s)\n         </span>\n        </th>\n        <td>\n         <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">\n          English\n         </a>\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <th scope="row">\n         Production\n         <span class="nowrap">\n          company(s)\n         </span>\n        </th>\n        <td>\n         <a href="/wiki/Grant_Tinker" title="Grant Tinker">\n          GTG East\n         </a>\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <th class="summary" colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background: #CCCCFF; padding:
